% Inherit from the specified cell style.




    
\documentclass[11pt]{article}

    
    
    \usepackage[T1]{fontenc}
    % Nicer default font (+ math font) than Computer Modern for most use cases
    \usepackage{mathpazo}

    % Basic figure setup, for now with no caption control since it's done
    % automatically by Pandoc (which extracts ![](path) syntax from Markdown).
    \usepackage{graphicx}
    % We will generate all images so they have a width \maxwidth. This means
    % that they will get their normal width if they fit onto the page, but
    % are scaled down if they would overflow the margins.
    \makeatletter
    \def\maxwidth{\ifdim\Gin@nat@width>\linewidth\linewidth
    \else\Gin@nat@width\fi}
    \makeatother
    \let\Oldincludegraphics\includegraphics
    % Set max figure width to be 80% of text width, for now hardcoded.
    \renewcommand{\includegraphics}[1]{\Oldincludegraphics[width=.8\maxwidth]{#1}}
    % Ensure that by default, figures have no caption (until we provide a
    % proper Figure object with a Caption API and a way to capture that
    % in the conversion process - todo).
    \usepackage{caption}
    \DeclareCaptionLabelFormat{nolabel}{}
    \captionsetup{labelformat=nolabel}

    \usepackage{adjustbox} % Used to constrain images to a maximum size 
    \usepackage{xcolor} % Allow colors to be defined
    \usepackage{enumerate} % Needed for markdown enumerations to work
    \usepackage{geometry} % Used to adjust the document margins
    \usepackage{amsmath} % Equations
    \usepackage{amssymb} % Equations
    \usepackage{textcomp} % defines textquotesingle
    % Hack from http://tex.stackexchange.com/a/47451/13684:
    \AtBeginDocument{%
        \def\PYZsq{\textquotesingle}% Upright quotes in Pygmentized code
    }
    \usepackage{upquote} % Upright quotes for verbatim code
    \usepackage{eurosym} % defines \euro
    \usepackage[mathletters]{ucs} % Extended unicode (utf-8) support
    \usepackage[utf8x]{inputenc} % Allow utf-8 characters in the tex document
    \usepackage{fancyvrb} % verbatim replacement that allows latex
    \usepackage{grffile} % extends the file name processing of package graphics 
                         % to support a larger range 
    % The hyperref package gives us a pdf with properly built
    % internal navigation ('pdf bookmarks' for the table of contents,
    % internal cross-reference links, web links for URLs, etc.)
    \usepackage{hyperref}
    \usepackage{longtable} % longtable support required by pandoc >1.10
    \usepackage{booktabs}  % table support for pandoc > 1.12.2
    \usepackage[inline]{enumitem} % IRkernel/repr support (it uses the enumerate* environment)
    \usepackage[normalem]{ulem} % ulem is needed to support strikethroughs (\sout)
                                % normalem makes italics be italics, not underlines

    \definecolor{urlcolor}{rgb}{0,.145,.698}

\def\PY@reset{\let\PY@it=\relax \let\PY@bf=\relax%
    \let\PY@ul=\relax \let\PY@tc=\relax%
    \let\PY@bc=\relax \let\PY@ff=\relax}
\def\PY@tok#1{\csname PY@tok@#1\endcsname}
\def\PY@toks#1+{\ifx\relax#1\empty\else%
    \PY@tok{#1}\expandafter\PY@toks\fi}
\def\PY@do#1{\PY@bc{\PY@tc{\PY@ul{%
    \PY@it{\PY@bf{\PY@ff{#1}}}}}}}
\def\PY#1#2{\PY@reset\PY@toks#1+\relax+\PY@do{#2}}

    \section{Data Preparation}\label{data-preparation}

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]

    \subsection{Feature engineering ans Feature
Selection}\label{feature-engineering-ans-feature-selection}

Feature engineering helps the learning algorithm to extract a stronger
signal from the data. It removes less relevant features and creates new
features from the existing data.

The credit card dataset does not have original features. It contains
only the principal components derived from PCA.

Feature selection is not necessary: the number of samples vastly
outnumber the number of features (284k vs 30), thus, overfitting is less
likely.

Let's produce a correlation matrix to make sure that the features only
slightly correlate to each other.

\subsubsection{Check correlation of
features}\label{check-correlation-of-features}

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
{\color{incolor}In [{\color{incolor}31}]:} \PY{n}{correlationMatrix} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{pd}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{DataFrame}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{data}\PY{o}{=}\PY{p}{[}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{index}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{dataX}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{columns}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{columns}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{dataX}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{columns}\PY{p}{)}

    \subsection{Model Preparation}\label{model-preparation}

    We need to split the data into a training and a test set, select a cost
function and prepare for k-fold cross-validation

    \subsubsection{Split into training and test
sets}\label{split-into-training-and-test-sets}

values in the sample produced will be the same as the proportion of
values provided to parameter \texttt{stratify}.

    \subsubsection{Selecting the cost
function}\label{selecting-the-cost-function}

    The algorithm will learn by minimizing the loss function. The current
dataset is a classification task with two classes. Thus, we can use
\textbf{binary classification\_log\_loss}:

\[
logloss = -\frac{1}{N}\sum_{i=1}^{N}\sum_{j=1}^{M}y_{i,j}log(p_{i,j})
\]

where \(N\) is the number of observations; \(M\) is the number of class
labels (2 in our case); \(log\) is the natural algorithm; \(y_{i, j}\)
is 1 if observation \(i\) is in class \(j\) and 0 otherwise;
\(p_{i, j}\) is the predicted probability that observation \(i\) is in
class \(j\).

The algoritm will generate the class probabilities for observations. The
closer the probabilities are to true labels, the lower will be the value
of the loss function.

    \subsubsection{Create k-Fold cross-validation
sets}\label{create-k-fold-cross-validation-sets}

To improve the performance of the algorithm on never-before-seen
samples, the training set will be split further into a training and a
validation set. The data is split into 5 parts. The training is done on
4 fifth and validated on the 5th part. This will be repeated 5 times, so
we will get 5 estimates of the generalization error. The predictions
generated fot the validation part will be stored as well as the training
and the validation scores for each of the 5 runs. At the end of all
runs, we will have validation predictions for all observations in the
dataset. This will be the all-in estimate of the performance.

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
{\color{incolor}In [{\color{incolor}47}]:} \PY{n}{k\PYZus{}fold} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{StratifiedKFold}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{n\PYZus{}splits}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mi}{5}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{shuffle}\PY{o}{=}\PY{k+kc}{True}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{random\PYZus{}state}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mi}{2018}\PY{p}{)}
\end{Verbatim}


    \section{Machine Learning Models}\label{machine-learning-models}

    \subsection{Model 1: Logistic
Regression}\label{model-1-logistic-regression}

Logistic regression is the most basic classification algorithm

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
{\color{incolor}In [{\color{incolor}56}]:} \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} set up hyperparams}
         
         \PY{n}{penalty} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{l2}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}
         \PY{n}{C} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+m+mf}{1.0}
         \PY{n}{class\PYZus{}weight} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{balanced}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}
         \PY{n}{solver} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{liblinear}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}
         \PY{n}{n\PYZus{}jobs} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+m+mi}{1}
         \PY{n}{random\PYZus{}state} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+m+mi}{2018}
         
         \PY{n}{logReg} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{LogisticRegression}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{penalty}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{penalty}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{C}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{C}\PY{p}{,}
                                     \PY{n}{class\PYZus{}weight}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{class\PYZus{}weight}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{random\PYZus{}state}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{random\PYZus{}state}\PY{p}{,}
                                    \PY{n}{solver}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{solver}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{n\PYZus{}jobs}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{n\PYZus{}jobs}\PY{p}{)}
\end{Verbatim}


    Note that the penalty is set to \(L2\) instead of \(L1\). \textbf{L2} is
less sensitive to outliers and will assign non-zero weights to all
features. This will bring a more stable solution. \textbf{L1}, on the
other hand, will assign high weights to the most important features and
near-zero weight to all the rest, effectibely doing feature selection.
However, since the weights vary so much from feature to feature,
\textbf{L1} will produce a solution that is not as stable to changes in
data points. More details on the choice between the two norms here:
http://www.chioka.in/differences-between-l1-and-l2-as-loss-function-and-regularization/

\textbf{C} is the strength of the regulariztion. The smaller the number,
the stronger is the regularization.

\textbf{class\_weight} is set to 'balanced' because the dataset is very
imbalanced. This tells the algorithm to focus on learning from
positively labeled transactions (i.e., the fraudulent ones)

\paragraph{Train the model}\label{train-the-model}

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
{\color{incolor}In [{\color{incolor}59}]:} \PY{n}{training\PYZus{}scores} \PY{o}{=} \PY{p}{[}\PY{p}{]}
         \PY{n}{cv\PYZus{}scores} \PY{o}{=} \PY{p}{[}\PY{p}{]}
         \PY{n}{predictionsBasedOnKFolds} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{pd}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{DataFrame}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{data}\PY{o}{=}\PY{p}{[}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{,}
                                                \PY{n}{index}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{y\PYZus{}train}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{index}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{columns}\PY{o}{=}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{,}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{)}
         
         \PY{n}{model} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{logReg}
         
         \PY{k}{for} \PY{n}{train\PYZus{}index}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{cv\PYZus{}index} \PY{o+ow}{in} \PY{n}{k\PYZus{}fold}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{split}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{zeros}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n+nb}{len}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{X\PYZus{}train}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} 
                                                  \PY{n}{y\PYZus{}train}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{ravel}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{:}
             \PY{n}{X\PYZus{}train\PYZus{}fold}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{X\PYZus{}cv\PYZus{}fold} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{X\PYZus{}train}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{iloc}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{train\PYZus{}index}\PY{p}{,}\PY{p}{:}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{,} \PYZbs{}
                 \PY{n}{X\PYZus{}train}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{iloc}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{cv\PYZus{}index}\PY{p}{,} \PY{p}{:}\PY{p}{]}
             
             \PY{n}{y\PYZus{}train\PYZus{}fold}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{y\PYZus{}cv\PYZus{}fold} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{y\PYZus{}train}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{iloc}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{train\PYZus{}index}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{,} \PYZbs{}
                 \PY{n}{y\PYZus{}train}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{iloc}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{cv\PYZus{}index}\PY{p}{]}
             
             \PY{n}{model}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{fit}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{X\PYZus{}train\PYZus{}fold}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{y\PYZus{}train\PYZus{}fold}\PY{p}{)}
             \PY{n}{loglossTraining} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{log\PYZus{}loss}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{y\PYZus{}train\PYZus{}fold}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{model}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{predict\PYZus{}proba}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{X\PYZus{}train\PYZus{}fold}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{[}\PY{p}{:}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{)}
             \PY{n}{training\PYZus{}scores}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{append}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{loglossTraining}\PY{p}{)}
             
             \PY{n}{predictionsBasedOnKFolds}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{loc}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{X\PYZus{}cv\PYZus{}fold}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{index}\PY{p}{,} \PY{p}{:}\PY{p}{]} \PY{o}{=} \PYZbs{}
                 \PY{n}{model}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{predict\PYZus{}proba}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{X\PYZus{}cv\PYZus{}fold}\PY{p}{)}
             
             \PY{n}{loglossCV} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{log\PYZus{}loss}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{y\PYZus{}cv\PYZus{}fold}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{predictionsBasedOnKFolds}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{loc}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{X\PYZus{}cv\PYZus{}fold}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{index}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{)}
             \PY{n}{cv\PYZus{}scores}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{append}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{loglossCV}\PY{p}{)}
             
             \PY{n+nb}{print}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{Training Log Loss: }\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{loglossTraining}\PY{p}{)}
             \PY{n+nb}{print}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{CV Log Loss: }\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{loglossCV}\PY{p}{)}
             
         \PY{n}{loglossLogisticRegression} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{log\PYZus{}loss}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{y\PYZus{}train}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{predictionsBasedOnKFolds}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{loc}\PY{p}{[}\PY{p}{:}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{)}
         \PY{n+nb}{print}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{Logistic Regression Log Loss: }\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{loglossLogisticRegression}\PY{p}{)}
\end{Verbatim}

    \subsubsection{Evaluating the results}\label{evaluating-the-results}

The cross validation loss is higher than the training; that is expected.
The loss values are close. The algorithm does not significantly overfit.

We need a more intuitive way to interpret the results. The performace of
the model is evaluated on how well it distinguishes between good and
fraulutent transactions. That is, the closer are the predicted
probability of the class to its true label, the better.

We need to know, of the given fraudulent transactions, how many were
caught? This metric is \textbf{recall}.

\[
recall  = \frac{N_{true \ positive}}{N_{true \ positive} + N_{false \ negative}}
\]

Also, out of transaction predicted as fraudulent, how many were actually
fraudulent? This metric is \textbf{precision}.

\[
precision  = \frac{N_{true \ positive}}{N_{true \ positive} + N_{false \ positive}}
\]

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
{\color{incolor}In [{\color{incolor}62}]:} \PY{n}{preds} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{pd}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{concat}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{y\PYZus{}train}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{predictionsBasedOnKFolds}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{loc}\PY{p}{[}\PY{p}{:}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{axis}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{)}
         \PY{n}{preds}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{columns} \PY{o}{=} \PY{p}{[}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{trueLabel}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{prediction}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{]}
         \PY{n}{predictionsBasedOnKFoldsLogisticRegression} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{preds}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{copy}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}
\end{Verbatim}


    \paragraph{Confusion matrix}\label{confusion-matrix}

Our dataset is very imbalanced. Tha is, even if we classify all
transactions as non-fraudulent, 284315 true negatives, 492 false
negatives, 9 true positive, and 0 true false positives. This is not
really intuitive and does not reveal the true performance of the
algorithm on the highly imbalanced dataset.

We need a better way to evaluate performance.

\paragraph{Precision and Recall
Tradeoff}\label{precision-and-recall-tradeoff}

For the current task, it is important to have a balance between the
precision and recall. On one hand, we need high precision, so that as
few a possible cases are labeled as fraudulent when they are not. We do
not want to annoy honest customers with mistaken rejections. On the
other hand, we do not want to miss fraudulent transactions and need as
many fraud cases as possible captured.

To choose an optimal balnce, we can use some special metrics.

\paragraph{Receiver Operating
Charateristic}\label{receiver-operating-charateristic}

The \_\_ Receiver Operating Charateristic\_\_ is a curve plotted so that
on its Y axis we put the true positives rate (sensitivity) and on the X
axis - the false positives rate (1-specificity). This means, the higher
the value on the Y axis and the lower the value on the X axis - the
better the solution.

The ideal would be a point with coordinates 0.0, 1. The quality of the
solution can be measured by the area under the ROC curve (auROC). The
larger the area, the better the solution.

Let's plot the \textbf{precision-recall curve} and get the average
precision

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
{\color{incolor}In [{\color{incolor}65}]:} \PY{n}{average\PYZus{}precision} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{average\PYZus{}precision\PYZus{}score}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{preds}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{trueLabel}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{preds}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{prediction}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{)}
         \PY{n}{precision}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{recall}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{thressholds} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{precision\PYZus{}recall\PYZus{}curve}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{preds}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{trueLabel}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{preds}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{prediction}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{)}
\end{Verbatim}

    \adjustimage{max size={0.9\linewidth}{0.9\paperheight}}{output_50_1.png}
    \end{center}
    { \hspace*{\fill} \\}
    
    By moving along the Precision-Recall curve we can see how we can trade
precision for recall. For example, if we need to catch
\textasciitilde{}80\% of the fraud cases (80\% recall) we will end up
with \textasciitilde{}70\% precision, that is, 30\% of caught cases
would be unfairly labeled fraudulent.

Now, let's build the \textbf{ROC curve} and calculate the area under it:

    \subsection{Model 2: Random Forests}\label{model-2-random-forests}

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]

         \PY{n}{RFC} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{RandomForestClassifier}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{n\PYZus{}estimators}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{n\PYZus{}estimators}\PY{p}{,}
                                      \PY{n}{max\PYZus{}features}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{max\PYZus{}features}\PY{p}{,}     
                                      \PY{n}{max\PYZus{}depth}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{max\PYZus{}depth}\PY{p}{,}
                                      \PY{n}{min\PYZus{}samples\PYZus{}split}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{min\PYZus{}samples\PYZus{}split}\PY{p}{,}
                                      \PY{n}{min\PYZus{}samples\PYZus{}leaf}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{min\PYZus{}samples\PYZus{}leaf}\PY{p}{,}
                                      \PY{n}{max\PYZus{}leaf\PYZus{}nodes}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{max\PYZus{}leaf\PYZus{}nodes}\PY{p}{,}
                                      \PY{n}{min\PYZus{}weight\PYZus{}fraction\PYZus{}leaf}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{min\PYZus{}weight\PYZus{}fraction\PYZus{}leaf}\PY{p}{,}
                                      \PY{n}{bootstrap}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{bootstrap}\PY{p}{,}
                                      \PY{n}{oob\PYZus{}score}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{oob\PYZus{}score}\PY{p}{,}
                                      \PY{n}{n\PYZus{}jobs}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{n\PYZus{}jobs}\PY{p}{,}
                                      \PY{n}{random\PYZus{}state}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{random\PYZus{}state}\PY{p}{,}
                                      \PY{n}{class\PYZus{}weight}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{class\PYZus{}weight}\PY{p}{)}
\end{Verbatim}


    The number of estimators is set to 10. This means, we will build 10
trees and average the results accross them.

The number of features the dataset has is 30. With the
\texttt{max\_features} parameter set to 'auto', for each tree the model
will consider the square root of features (5, that is the square root of
30 rounded down)

The \texttt{max\_depth} is set to max. This will allow the model to grow
the tree as deep as possible splitting as mus as possible, given the
number of features.

The \texttt{class\_weight} is set to 'balanced' because the dataset we
have is unbalanced.

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]

         \PY{n}{predictionsBasedOnKFolds} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{pd}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{DataFrame}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{data}\PY{o}{=}\PY{p}{[}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{,}

    \subsubsection{Evaluating the Results}\label{evaluating-the-results}

This Random Forests model tends to overfit much stronger than the
Logistic Regression: notice the spread between log loss in the trainign
and valiations sets.

However, the total log loss in the model is about 10\% of the log loss
of the Logistic Regresssion. Despite the overfitting, the Random Forests
model works better for the credit card fraud detection,

As before, lets build the \textbf{precision-recall} curve and the
\textbf{ROC} curve.

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]

    The average precision we achieve with Random Forests is 0.79 which is
higher that 0.73 with the Logistic Regression. However, the auROC metric
is lower: 0.93 vs 0.97

    \subsection{Model 3: Gradient Boosting machine
(XGBoost)}\label{model-3-gradient-boosting-machine-xgboost}

We now try gradient boosting for our problem. We can use two models,
XGBoost and a faster vesrion by Microsoft - LightGBM.

We start with XGBoost.

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
{\color{incolor}In [{\color{incolor}86}]:} \PY{n}{params\PYZus{}xGB} \PY{o}{=} \PY{p}{\PYZob{}}
             \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{nthread}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{:}\PY{l+m+mi}{16}\PY{p}{,} \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{}number of cores}
             \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{learning\PYZus{}rate}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{:}\PY{l+m+mf}{0.3}\PY{p}{,} \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} range 0 to 1, default 0.3}
             \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{gamma}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{:} \PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{,} \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} range 0 to infinity, default 0}
                 \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} increase to reduce complexity (increase bias, reduce variance)}
             \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{max\PYZus{}depth}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{:} \PY{l+m+mi}{6}\PY{p}{,} \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} range 1 to infinity, default 6}
             \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{min\PYZus{}child\PYZus{}weight}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{:} \PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{,} \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} range 0 to infinity, default 1}
             \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{max\PYZus{}delta\PYZus{}step}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{:} \PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{,} \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{}range 0 to infinity, default 0}
             \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{subsample}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{:} \PY{l+m+mf}{1.0}\PY{p}{,} \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} range 0 to 1, default 1}
                 \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} subsample ratio of the training examples}
             \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{colsample\PYZus{}bytree}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{:} \PY{l+m+mf}{1.0}\PY{p}{,} \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} range 0 to 1, default 1}
                 \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} subsample ratio of features}
             \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{objective}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{:} \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{binary:logistic}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,}
             \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{num\PYZus{}class}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{:} \PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{,}
             \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{eval\PYZus{}metric}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{:} \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{logloss}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,}
             \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{seed}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{:}\PY{l+m+mi}{2018}\PY{p}{,}
             \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{silent}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{:} \PY{l+m+mi}{1}
         \PY{p}{\PYZcb{}}
\end{Verbatim}


    We've set up the binary classification using log loss as the cost
function. The max tree pepth is 6 and the default learning rate is 0.3.
For each tree we will use all observations and all features.

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]

[50]	train-logloss:0.0001504+9.8102e-06	test-logloss:0.0032568+0.000691139
[100]	train-logloss:6e-05+2.28035e-06	test-logloss:0.0035872+0.000779951
[150]	train-logloss:4.36e-05+1.49666e-06	test-logloss:0.0036988+0.000804066
[200]	train-logloss:3.68e-05+1.16619e-06	test-logloss:0.003785+0.000828
Training Log Loss:  0.0009232458662582204
CV Log Loss:  0.0026206647420319276
XGBoost Gradient Boosting Log Loss:  0.0029566664839003366

    \end{Verbatim}

    For each of the five rounds, the model will train for as many as 2000
rounds. It will evaluate whether the log log is is decreasing. If the
crosvalidation stops improving over the last 200 rounds, the process
will stop to avoid overfitting.

\subsubsection{Evaluate the Results}\label{evaluate-the-results}

The log loss of the \textbf{XGBoost Gradient Boosting} is
0.0029566664839003366. It is 20\% of the result of the Random Forests
model and 2\% oof the log loss returned by the logistic regression.

Let's see, how do the precision-recall and ROC curves look like:

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]

    \subsection{Model 4. Gradient Boosting Machine
(LightGBM)}\label{model-4.-gradient-boosting-machine-lightgbm}

Setting the hyperparameters

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]

              \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{application}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{:}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{binary}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,}
              \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{num\PYZus{}class}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{:} \PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{,}
              \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{boosting}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{:}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{gbdt}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,}
              \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{objective}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{:} \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{binary}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,}
              \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{metric}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{:} \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{binary\PYZus{}logloss}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,}
              \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{metric\PYZus{}freq}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{:} \PY{l+m+mi}{50}\PY{p}{,}
              \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{is\PYZus{}training\PYZus{}metric}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{:}\PY{k+kc}{False}\PY{p}{,}
              \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{max\PYZus{}depth}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{:}\PY{l+m+mi}{4}\PY{p}{,}
              \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{num\PYZus{}leaves}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{:} \PY{l+m+mi}{31}\PY{p}{,}
              \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{learning\PYZus{}rate}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{:} \PY{l+m+mf}{0.01}\PY{p}{,}
              \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{feature\PYZus{}fraction}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{:}\PY{l+m+mf}{1.0}\PY{p}{,}
              \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{bagging\PYZus{}fraction}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{:}\PY{l+m+mf}{1.0}\PY{p}{,}
              \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{bagging\PYZus{}freq}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{:} \PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{,}
              \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{bagging\PYZus{}seed}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{:} \PY{l+m+mi}{2018}\PY{p}{,}
              \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{verbose}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{:} \PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{,}
              \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{num\PYZus{}threads}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{:} \PY{l+m+mi}{16}
          \PY{p}{\PYZcb{}}
\end{Verbatim}


    \subsubsection{Train the model}\label{train-the-model}

We will perform the \emph{k}-fold cross-validation and go over it 5
times, storing the results.

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
{\color{incolor}In [{\color{incolor}109}]:} \PY{n}{trainingScores} \PY{o}{=} \PY{p}{[}\PY{p}{]}
          \PY{n}{cvScores} \PY{o}{=} \PY{p}{[}\PY{p}{]}
          \PY{n}{predictionsBasedOnKFolds} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{pd}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{DataFrame}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{data}\PY{o}{=}\PY{p}{[}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{,}

                  \PY{n}{X\PYZus{}train}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{iloc}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{cv\PYZus{}index}\PY{p}{,}\PY{p}{:}\PY{p}{]}

              \PY{n}{lgb\PYZus{}eval} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{lgb}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{Dataset}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{X\PYZus{}cv\PYZus{}fold}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{y\PYZus{}cv\PYZus{}fold}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{reference}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{lgb\PYZus{}train}\PY{p}{)}
              \PY{n}{gbm} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{lgb}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{train}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{params\PYZus{}lightGB}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{lgb\PYZus{}train}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{num\PYZus{}boost\PYZus{}round}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mi}{2000}\PY{p}{,}
                             \PY{n}{valid\PYZus{}sets}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{lgb\PYZus{}eval}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{early\PYZus{}stopping\PYZus{}rounds}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mi}{200}\PY{p}{)}
              
              \PY{n}{loglossTraining} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{log\PYZus{}loss}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{y\PYZus{}train\PYZus{}fold}\PY{p}{,} \PYZbs{}

                  \PY{n}{gbm}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{predict}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{X\PYZus{}cv\PYZus{}fold}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{num\PYZus{}iteration}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{gbm}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{best\PYZus{}iteration}\PY{p}{)}

                  \PY{n}{predictionsBasedOnKFolds}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{loc}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{X\PYZus{}cv\PYZus{}fold}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{index}\PY{p}{,}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{prediction}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{)}

    \subsubsection{Evaluate the results}\label{evaluate-the-results}

From the data below, we can see the the loss values of the LightGBM are
similar to that of XGBoost. They are also 20\% of the random forests and
2\% of the logistic regression. In comparison to XGboost, LightGMB is
much faster.

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]

dataset and compare the loss function values.

    { \hspace*{\fill} \\}
    
    With LightGBM we can catch over 80\% of fraudulent transactions with
90\% precision. Given the rarity of fraudulent transactions in the
dataset, this is an impressive result.

    \subsection{Ensemble}\label{ensemble}

We can combine several models to improve performance. There are,
however, a few rules.

For the best result, the models in the ensemble must be strong and from
unrelated families, i.e. uncorrelated.

If one of the models is much better than the others, then the weaker
models will not contribute must to the improvement, The end result will
not be better than that of the the strongest model.

If the models in the ensemble are correlated, they will not be better
together that in standalone. Their weaknesses will not be compensated.

In our case, two models (XGBoost and LightGMB) are much better hat the
two others. But the best models also belong to the same family. Because
of this we cannot hope on any significant improvements of a model
ensemble.

Let's prove this.

\subsubsection{Stacking}\label{stacking}

Stacking is a form of ensemble. In stacking, we take the prediction done
by one model on its \emph{k}-fold validation (\textbf{layer one
oredictions}) and append them to the original dataset, The new dataset
is then used to train the second model using \emph{k}-fold
cross-validation.

The result is the \textbf{layer two} predictions that we will use to
evaluate the ensemble and see it it performs better than the standalone
models.

\subsubsection{Combine layer one predictions with the original
dataset}\label{combine-layer-one-predictions-with-the-original-dataset}

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]

    As expected, we do not see any improvement. The best standalone
solutions come from the same family and share strengths and weaknesses.
They do not complement each other.

Let's take a look at the precision-recall and auROC curves:

    \subsection{Final Model Selection}\label{final-model-selection}

We tries an ensemble method but it did not bring any imrovement. To make
thing sinpler, we staywith the best standalone models, LightGMB.

Let's visualize, how LightGMBH separates the fraudulent cases from the
good ones.

    \adjustimage{max size={0.9\linewidth}{0.9\paperheight}}{output_100_0.png}

    { \hspace*{\fill} \\}
    
    In the right side of this plot, we see all the fraudulent cases. Most of
them receive high probability from the predictor, with few exceptions of
false negatives in the bottom right corner. In the left side, we see
non-fraudulent cases and most of them correctly receive a low
probability score from the predictor.

    \subsection{Production Pipeline}\label{production-pipeline}

Now that we have selected the model, we can desine a pipeline that
performs three steps:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  loads the incoming data
\item
  scales the features
\item
  geenrates predictions using the selected model
\end{itemize}

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
{\color{incolor}In [{\color{incolor}157}]:} \PY{l+s+sd}{\PYZsq{}\PYZsq{}\PYZsq{}Pipeline for New Data\PYZsq{}\PYZsq{}\PYZsq{}}
          \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} first, import new data into a dataframe called \PYZsq{}newData\PYZsq{}}
          \PY{n}{newData} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{dataX}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{copy}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}
          
          \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} second, scale data}
          \PY{n}{newData}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{loc}\PY{p}{[}\PY{p}{:}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{featuresToScale}\PY{p}{]} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{sX}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{transform}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{newData}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{featuresToScale}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{)}
          
          \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} third, predict using LightGBM}
          \PY{n}{gbm}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{predict}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{newData}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{num\PYZus{}iteration}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{gbm}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{best\PYZus{}iteration}\PY{p}{)}
\end{Verbatim}


\begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
{\color{outcolor}Out[{\color{outcolor}157}]:} array([7.02910929e-05, 6.22685906e-05, 1.93665049e-04, {\ldots},
                 1.45178021e-04, 9.60438864e-05, 6.22685906e-05])
\end{Verbatim}
            
    This model needs to be retrained becasue the fraud patterns may change.

An improvement to this system would be an algorithm from an unrelated
family that would have a similar performance. Then we could combine the
two into an ensemble for better results.
